Real Estate Desk; 11
The Price of 'Wow!' Keeps On Rising
By NADINE BROZAN
2837 words
7 September 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
Copyright 2003 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

THERE was a time when Kirk Henckels, director of private brokerage for the tony firm of Stribling & Associates, would refer to an apartment on the market for $10 million as ''a trophy property.'' No more.

''Now I just call it a $10 million property,'' Mr. Henckels remarked. The term trophy does not enter his vocabulary until the asking price hits $20 million.

The boundary between ultrahigh luxury prices and routine high prices depends, of course, on who's drawing the line and from what vantage point. But by and large, brokers, developers and appraisers agree that it lies somewhere north of $10 million.

''It used to be that you would walk into a $10 million property and it would be like seeing someone wearing a 25-carat engagement ring,'' said Michele Kleier, president of Gumley Haft Kleier. ''Now you look at something at $10 million and say, 'Where do I start gutting?' and 'How many more millions do I have to put in?' ''

Jonathan Miller, president of Miller Samuel, a firm that appraises about 5,000 properties a year, also sets the bar higher. ''I think that $10 million is not high enough for the 'Wow' factor,'' he said. ''It's probably more like $12 million to $15 million.''

But there is ''wow,'' and there is ''Wow!'' To Mr. Henckels, '' 'wow' with a small 'w' is $10 million to $20 million while capital 'W' 'Wow' is over $20 million.''

Wherever ''wow'' rests, it was certainly uttered more than once when word spread about a $45 million transaction -- the highest ever recorded in Manhattan -- in the AOL Time Warner Center on Columbus Circle. The purchase of a gargantuan (12,000 square feet) loft assembled by combining one entire floor and part of another there remains breathtaking, even for movie stars and corporate tycoons. The buyer was David Martinez, a London-based financier who made his fortune buying the debt of nations and companies.

But he remains in a class by himself. As Stephen Kotler, executive vice president of Douglas Elliman, put it, ''There is no rash of $45 million buyers.''

If there were, they might find some latitude on asking prices, which slipped noticeably in the upper reaches last fall, Mr. Miller said. But that was due more to overpricing, he said, than to real decline. ''List prices were being set as if the market were continuing to see double digit increases,'' he said. ''Now we are seeing prices at this level remaining flat, but there is a modest uptick in the number of transactions.''

There is a good inventory of properties for people able to bid $10 million or more. Mr. Kotler conducted an informal online survey of $10-million-plus apartments last week. Above that cutoff, he said, ''our database shows there are 144 available apartments, with an average asking price of $16,286,000 for an average of $2,680 per square foot. The average square footage of these units is 6,100.''

(A survey done by Mr. Miller for Douglas Elliman shows the average sales price in Manhattan over all for the second quarter of 2003 at $660 a square foot.)

Some specialists believe that price per square foot is a more sensitive barometer of high-end luxury than absolute price. ''At $1,000 per square foot, you get something very nice; $1,500, something extravagant; and $2,000 plus, hotel quality service, concierges, parking, five-star restaurants with catering and usually premium addresses with views,'' said Bret Bobo, senior vice president of sales and marketing for the Athena Group, a development firm.

So what can buyers crossing the $10-million-and-up threshold expect to get that they would not for a single-digit million? ''Five million dollars or $6 million can buy you a great location, a terrific apartment, a beautiful home, but they are not palaces,'' said Hall F. Willkie, president of Brown Harris Stevens Residential Sales. ''It's like the difference between buying a Mercedes versus a Rolls-Royce.''

In general, Ms. Kleier said, ''What you're getting is more space, larger rooms, higher ceilings and views. If you're buying at Trump World Tower or the AOL Time Warner building and you're spending that kind of money, you won't buy on the 15th floor but on the 70th. You want to walk in and have drop-dead airplane views. If you are buying in a prewar co-op,'' she said, ''you will be getting something that will never be built again, an Old World building with snob appeal.''

No matter how unique any particular residence may be, real estate specialists agree that there are three essential factors in catapulting a property into the megamillion range:

*Location.

*Size.

*View.

The way those factors balance one another is different for stately old co-ops (for which location, and all that it implies, is paramount) than they are for glamorous new condos (views are essential). For town houses, width -- more than 20 feet -- dominates all other considerations.

Cliché though it may be, there seems no way to overstate the pull of location, especially in the co-op sector.

The most expensive -- and exclusive -- buildings sit on Fifth and Park Avenues from 59th to 96th Streets. Although there are certainly other neighborhoods and streets with prestige, like Sutton and Beekman Places, Central Park South, Central Park West and Gramercy Park, none command quite the same cachet.

Some buildings on Fifth and Park are known simply by their numbers. ''People don't say '740 Park Avenue,' they just say '740' and no one asks '740 what?' '' Ms. Kleier said. ''Everyone knows you are worth a billion dollars or you wouldn't get through the board. It is the prestige of having that address on your stationery.''

Though many consider Central Park West to be as desirable as Fifth and Park, ''there are not as many really expensive apartments there,'' said Dolly Lenz, executive vice president of Douglas Elliman. ''For the most part, you only get humongous apartments when they are combined. On the East Side, they were built huge.''

In the town house market, the gap in prices between East and West Sides has been narrowing in recent years. ''But they are still 25 percent higher on the Upper East Side blocks between Fifth and Madison,'' said Jed Garfield, the managing partner at Leslie J. Garfield & Company.

There are, to be sure, some multimillion dollar properties downtown as well, but not in the concentration of the Upper East Side.

Still, it takes more than a high-status address to escalate prices. ''It must be an apartment in a very fine building,'' said Kathy Sloane, a broker at Brown Harris Stevens, ''fine being defined as address, original architect and designer and provenance, meaning the people who have lived there. If the building is by Rosario Candela or James Carpenter, you know it will have certain ceiling heights, a scale that creates a sense of graciousness, sufficient attention to areas for servants, a very fine lobby and a handsome exterior and possibly a terrace.''

To Mr. Kotler, size is paramount. ''You can get something as nice for $6 million or $7 million with the same level of finishes, but the difference comes down to size.'' he said.

The most recent buzz around Fifth and Park concerned the contract reportedly signed for the duplex at 770 Park Avenue owned by Giovanni Agnelli, the chairman of Fiat and elder statesman of Italian business, who died in January.

''It is not big, 6,000 feet, but it is one of the best apartments in New York,'' said Alice Mason, a broker who was not involved in the sale. ''There were 22 appointments to see it the first week it came on the market.'' Sotheby's, the brokerage firm handling the sale, declined to comment.

Though the duplex is rumored to have gone for $20 million, ''in that same building you can find something for $6 million,'' said Roger Erickson, senior managing director of William B. May. ''They share a location. What they don't share is the location within the building and that, along with size, is the key factor that vaults you to $15 million.''

The fact that the stately prewar co-op buildings, particularly those by revered architects like Candela and Carpenter, cannot be expanded or duplicated, adds to their mystique and their value.

''You can't replace the prewar buildings on Park that were built in the 1920's,'' Ms. Mason said. ''The location is 100 percent gone and the type of craftsmanship used in the old buildings no longer exists.''

That is what makes the building at 838 Fifth Avenue, at 65th Street, unusual. Known as the ''Love Thy Neighbor'' building for the biblical injunction carved into its limestone facade for its previous owner, the Union of American Hebrew Congregations, it is both a condo in a sea of co-ops, and a gut renovation. (According to Lawrence Sicular, director of appraisal and consulting for Brown Harris Stevens, there are 63 co-ops and five condos on Fifth Avenue between 59th and 96th Street.)

Among the amenities at 838 Fifth, which commanded $10 million to $20 million per apartment: quarters on the ground floor for employees. ''With square footage so expensive upstairs, over a million per bedroom, it was decided that staff didn't have to live in the same apartment,'' Mr. Bobo said. ''So we put in one staff room for each floor, and they sold out at $500,000 each.''

Not all the residents house staff there, however. ''One owner put in a gym, another a wine cellar and storage,'' said Mr. Bobo, whose company converted the building to residential use.

The differences between co-ops and condos, of course, range from the basic -- condominiums, like houses, are real property, while co-ops are corporations in which tenants own shares, with rules, and admission, in the hands of a board -- to the subtle. In Old World co-ops, ''there is a snobbery that no condo can have,'' Ms. Mason said. ''A condo is only about money, and these buildings are only partly about money.''

Dolly Lenz, executive vice president and managing director of Douglas Elliman, disagreed. ''People used to think you were odd if you were a New Yorker and said you wanted to buy a condo,'' she said. ''Now, thanks to buildings like the Westbury at 15 East 69th Street and 838 Fifth Avenue, it is not just acceptable but preferable to be in a condo because of the incredible people who have bought there.''

Ultraluxury condominiums can also be found beyond -- in some cases well beyond -- traditionally affluent neighborhoods. They can even bestow a new identity on their locations, as the twin Richard Meier buildings, at 173 and 176 Perry Street where prices ranged from $1.6 million to $18 million, have done on Perry Street and One Beacon Court is expected to do on Lexington Avenue. At One Beacon Court, part of the development on the old Alexander's store site, prices will top out at $26 million.

''Some buildings make the location,'' Ms. Kleier said. ''Who would have thought people would be fighting to live on Columbus Circle?''

To compete with co-ops, Mr. Sicular said, condos must offer newness and extraordinary services and amenities. ''Co-ops have personnel but not the bells-and-whistles amenities of pools and, with a few exceptions, in-house restaurants,'' he said. ''While many high end co-ops have views, many don't, and Park Avenue is a premier example of that. Condos tend to need a view.''

It would be hard to find views more spectacular than those visible from the two behemoths soaring into the skyline across town from one another: the AOL Time Warner Center at Columbus Circle and One Beacon Court at 151 East 58th Street.

Prices in the AOL Time Warner Center start at $2.5 million (for a two-bedroom 1,283-square-foot unit facing west). The largest single sale there registered at $30 million. Though Mr. Martinez's purchase was for $45 million, he bought two units.

The towers, one to be known, because of the hotel at its base, as the Residences at Mandarin Oriental (65 apartments) and the other as One Central Park (135 apartments), are equal in height to a building 80 stories tall but because of the unusually high ceilings, and the retail complex at their bases, they actually have only 55 floors.

''This is five star living, and money is no object,'' said Louise M. Sunshine, president of the real estate marketing company bearing her name. The developer has trademarked the phrase ''five star living.''

THE aim, Ms. Sunshine said, is to provide a remarkable array of services and facilities and be ready to fulfill any request, no matter how challenging. As the promotional literature breathlessly says, ''Discover how expectations are surpassed; whether it's 3,000 roses upon request or monogrammed pillow shams for your houseguests, here the comforts of home have been exquisitely elevated.''

In Ms. Lenz's opinion, ''If you are marketing a new building today and don't have really great services, it's a hard sell. ''People are purchasing lifestyle. They can afford everything and want everything.''

And they get it at buildings like AOL Time Warner, One Beacon Court and the new Ritz-Carlton at 50 Central Park South. ''Any building can do maid service, but here they will dedicate the maid to your home,'' Ms. Lenz said of the Ritz-Carlton. ''If you want her, she must drop what she is doing. If there is a dog in residence, you get a dedicated dog walker who knows your dog.''

''There are two Bentley limos available,'' she continued, ''and they have fractional ownership of a jet, so if you decide you want to go to Palm Beach for the weekend, you can do that.''

Prices for 11 floor-through apartments were initially set at $15 million to $26 million, but one floor was split into two units priced at $12 million and $17 million after construction began. ''We decided we wanted to be able to offer something below $15 million,'' said Matthew Hall, spokesman for Millennium Partners, the developer.

Only the 23rd floor has completely finished interiors, with the rest offered as raw space. ''At this price point, buyers want their own architects and interior designers,'' Mr. Hall said. ''If we put in the finishes, we would never select the right ones.''

It is not unusual for buyers at this level to rip out kitchens and bathrooms and to redo finishes, but Donald Trump, who has put up some of the more opulent condos around town, said that purchasers tend to leave well enough alone in the kitchens of his Trump World Tower at 845 United Nations Plaza.

In an advertisement currently running in several magazines, Mr. Trump stands over a pizza in a box in a kitchen there equipped with G.E. Monogram appliances. The penthouse apartment is described as ''90th floor 10 rms w vu, New York at your feet $17 million.'' He is beaming as he holds out a slice. ''People very rarely tear out what I build,'' he said in an interview.

In town houses, services are virtually nonexistent and high end finishes may be attractive, but they do not have much impact on prices, according to Mr. Garfield, who said, ''There is not much difference between what you get for $8 million or $9 million and $15 million or $16 million. You can only spend so much on a house. There is no such thing as a Viking Plus range; a Viking is a Viking.''

What does count is easier to quantify: width. ''Twenty feet is standard,'' Mr. Garfield said. ''There are lots of buildings at 15, 18, 19 feet, but once you break the 20-foot barrier, you start moving up disproportionately. Plenty of multimillionaires have 20 feet but not many have 22 or 25.''

Whatever the extravagance of luxuries offered, at some point they become irrelevant. As Ms. Sunshine put it, ''They are for the $3 million to $10 million buyer, not the $45 million buyer. Those people come with their own luxuries. They have their own major-domos, drivers, concierges. If they want to exercise they put in their own gyms, but they still want to know that the building's gym is there.''

New Jersey Weekly Desk; SECT14NJ
HOUSING RULES REVISED
By George James
180 words
31 August 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
5
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

The state's Council on Affordable Housing proposed new rules last week intended to make it easier for municipalities to provide low- and moderate-income housing. Under the old system, each community had to have a fair share of lower-priced housing based on a complicated formula that many town officials criticized as cumbersome and developers used as a wedge to build large, dense housing complexes. Under the new rules, towns would have to require one new lower-priced unit for every 10 housing units built, and one unit for every 30 jobs created by new commercial construction. Critics questioned whether the new rules would have the desired effect or would result in keeping the poor and minorities in cities. They complained that the new rules allow towns that are not growing to avoid providing low- and moderate-income housing. Critics also complained that the rules doubled the number of units that could be set aside for the elderly, from 25 percent to 50 percent. George James

In the Region/Long Island
Real Estate Desk; SECT11
Demand for Low-Cost Homes Sparks Co-op Sales
By CAROLE PAQUETTE
1544 words
31 August 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
7
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

AS house prices on Long Island have soared, the starter home has become a thing of the past. So has the starter condominium. All that is left, housing experts say, is co-ops.

''Attached homes are the only viable first homes for entry-level workers,'' said Jim Morgo, president of the Long Island Housing Partnership, a nonprofit group based in Hauppauge. Condos, town houses and co-ops all used to be affordable, he said. Now only co-ops are.

''For the entry-level buyer, someone who is just starting their first job or buying their first house, the general rule for purchase is not to spend more than two and a half times the gross family income,'' or perhaps three times the income when interest rates are extraordinarily low, Mr. Morgo said. ''The median entry-level income for one person on Long Island is $56,000. You figure that for someone just starting a job the median could be $40,000, so they should not buy a house costing more than $120,000.'' Even the buyer earning $56,000 would in theory be able to afford a home costing no more than $168,000.

According to the Multiple Listing Service of Long Island, the median closing price for a home in Nassau in July was $400,000 and in Suffolk, $322,000. These prices reflect annual increases of more than 10 percent. A year earlier, the median in Nassau was $355,000, and in Suffolk, $273,000.

Prices of co-ops have increased as well, and the number sold has also grown sharply, with sales already past last year's total.

For 2003 through Aug. 1, 397 one-bedroom co-op units were sold in Nassau, with a median price of $172,000. In Suffolk, 154 co-op units were sold; the median price was $93,500. For all of last year, 343 one-bedroom units were sold in Nassau, with a median price of $149,000. In Suffolk, a total of 144 co-op units were sold with a median of $77,000.

Real estate agents say that in general condos, which are typically newer and have more amenities, sell for about 50 percent more than co-ops.

''As the market went up so have all types of housing,'' said Joseph Mottola, chief executive of the West Babylon-based Listing Service. ''Condos are traditionally priced higher than co-ops, and what is low end is always low end. What has changed is the definition of affordable housing. Co-ops generally are lower in price and affordable to whatever the market is. The prices today were unthinkable 10 years ago. The prices are still inching up to show what demand is there.''

Co-ops have always been the least expensive housing ''because of the type of ownership, in common with everyone else in the building,'' Mr. Morgo said. With a condominium, people own their own unit and pay real estate taxes; with a co-op, residents own shares in a corporation and the corporation pays the real estate taxes. A percentage of the monthly maintenance charge in a co-op is almost always tax deductible to reflect the corporate real-estate tax payments.

Co-ops are mainly converted rental apartments, said Roy Fedelem, the principal planner at the Suffolk County Planning Department. On Long Island, most conversions occurred in the early 1980's, he said, ''partly for financial reasons -- to give the owners of the building and the units some type of equity.''

''The buildings were old and had high carrying costs,'' he said. ''The interest rates were high at the time -- 12 to 19 percent -- and instead of refinancing an apartment the owner often converted to a co-op. In other words, he would sell in order to renovate.''

According to statistics provided by Peter Lambert, a senior planner for the county, 44 apartment complexes -- a total of 6,182 units -- were converted to co-ops in the 1980's in Suffolk's six western towns. In Nassau, a small number of conversions began in the 1960's and 1970's, but the majority -- 137 complexes with a total of 15,327 units -- were converted during the 1980's.

INCLUDING those built specifically as co-ops, there are now nearly 30,000 units on Long Island: 20,111 in Nassau and 9,644 in Suffolk.

Fairfield Properties, based in Commack, which manages 15 co-op complexes with a total of 2,000 units on Long Island, was one of the first to convert rental apartments to co-ops in Suffolk. In 1982, Fairfield bought and renovated an apartment complex in Nesconset that had been built in 1970 and created 36 co-op units called Smithwood.

The company bought seven additional rental properties, with about 800 units, for conversion in the 1980's, according to Mark A. Broxmeyer, the company's founder and a principal with his son, Michael, and brother, Gary.

''We found the values of apartments were going up, but rents were not keeping pace, so it didn't pay to keep them as rentals,'' Mark Broxmeyer said. ''Some of them were in deplorable condition.

''In the 1990's, when the economy changed and real estate went down, the co-op market was hit hard,'' he said. ''We had foreseen what was going to happen and had taken all the prospective co-op units off the market in the late 1980's. We kept them as rentals while we waited for the market to turn around. About two years ago it did, and we put the units back on the market.''

Since then, 200 of the 250 units that had been held back as rentals have been sold, he said.

In Suffolk, co-op prices range from $80,000 to $140,000 for a small one-bedroom to $110,000 to $150,000 for a two-bedroom and $199,000 for a three-bedroom, he said.

To illustrate what he called the ''humongous increase'' in co-op prices in the past few years, Michael Broxmeyer cited a 30-year-old 248-unit co-op complex in Patchogue.

''About five years ago, a one-bedroom had foreclosed for nonpayment of maintenance fees, and someone bought it for $10,000,'' Mr. Broxmeyer said. ''This year, he went to contract on the unit for $133,000.''

A large proportion of Nassau's co-ops are in Great Neck and Long Beach, which have a heavy concentration of apartment houses.

In Great Neck, 45 of the 60 apartment buildings are co-ops, according to Ellen Steinberg-Finkelstein, who specializes in co-op sales at Trylon Realty. ''The co-ops sell immediately,'' she said. ''Rentals and some condos, depending on the area, are very expensive.''

Rentals in the Great Neck area range from $1,650 to $2,200 a month and condos sell for $700,000 to more than $1 million for a two-bedroom, she said.

Co-ops sell from $75,000 to $130,000 for a studio; $140,000 to more than $500,000 for a one-bedroom; and $240,000 to $575,000 for a two-bedroom. Monthly maintenance charges range from $250 to $1,800. Ten years ago co-ops ranged from $40,000 to $160,000 for a one-bedroom unit, she said.

''Co-ops are popular with two types of buyers in Great Neck,'' said Lee Rothleder, president of the Long Island division of Goodstein Realty, a 76-year-old Manhattan company with three offices in Nassau -- two in Great Neck and one in Roslyn.

''Because of the 26-minute easy commute to Manhattan, the many co-ops near the Long Island Rail Road station in Great Neck Plaza attract the young couples with a new job that are just starting out,'' she said. ''We also see a huge amount of empty nesters who have just sold their home and are attracted by easy walking to the downtown, good parking at their buildings and our many parks.''

Houses in Great Neck range from $550,000 for a small three-bedroom to over $10 million for a large house on the waterfront. A one-bedroom condo starts at $300,000 and could run over $1 million, she said.

Joan Tramuta, a sales agent at the Plainview office of Prudential Douglas Elliman Realty, said: ''Before inflation you couldn't give a co-op away. Now with condos selling for up to 50 percent more than a co-op, they have become very popular and are considered affordable.''

''The co-ops go so fast, there are just not enough of them to sell,'' she said.

Ms. Tramuta noted that at Crest Wood Apartments in Bethpage -- a co-op that had once been a rental complex -- the asking price for a large one-bedroom apartment with a ''nice eat-in kitchen'' and an $842 monthly maintenance charge was $179,000. Within two days there had been multiple bids and the owner was offered $180,000.

Photo: At Crest Wood Apartments in Bethpage, the owner of a large one-bedroom co-op was offered $180,000. (Photo by Phil Marino for The New York Times)

Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Prices Climb Amid Record Sales of Homes
By Bloomberg News
559 words
26 August 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
4
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

Sales of previously owned homes rose to a record in July and prices recorded the biggest 12-month gain in almost 23 years, an industry report indicated yesterday.

Sales totaled 6.12 million at an annual pace, up 5 percent from June's 5.83 million pace and the third increase in the last four months, the report from the National Association of Realtors said. The increase was spurred by interest rates that in June had fallen to a low point in recent years. Economists had expected a 5.9-million-unit pace of home resales.

Mortgage rates have since risen, which may restrain home sales later this year and limit housing's contribution to economic recovery, economists said. While a rise of one percentage point in the average 30-year fixed rate since June may keep sales from strengthening, an accelerating economy will underpin the market for homes.

''It's likely that we have hit the peak,'' said Van Davis, president and chief executive of the Century 21 Real Estate Corporation. ''The good news is that I don't see a significant slowdown. The economy is picking up, real wages are rising and interest rates are still low, and in that environment,'' housing will not slump.

Existing-home sales are typically counted when the transactions close, meaning that the July statistics probably reflected buying decisions made a month or two earlier. The average rate on a 30-year mortgage reached 5.94 percent in July after falling in June to 5.21 percent, the lowest since the big mortgage buyer Freddie Mac started keeping records in 1971.

The lowest recorded 30-year rate was 5.83 percent in 1965, according to the Federal Housing Finance Board, which has figures back to 1963.

Home buying has related economic benefits, spurring purchases of costly items like furniture and appliances.

By region, resales rose 7.7 percent in the Northeast, to a 700,000-unit annual pace; 3.9 percent in the Midwest, to a pace of 1.34 million units; 7 percent in the South, to a 2.45-million-unit pace; and 1.9 percent in the West, to a pace of 1.63 million units.

The median price of a home rose 4.1 percent last month, to $182,100 from $175,000 in June, and more than 12 percent above the levels of July 2002. That was the biggest 12-month increase since November 1980. David Lereah, chief economist at the Realtors' association, said he expected the rise in home prices to slow to a range of 4 percent to 5 percent over the next 12 months.

The supply of homes available at the current sales pace fell to 4.7 months' worth in July from a 5.1-month supply in June.

Previously owned homes account for 85 percent of the residential real estate market, and new homes the rest.

The Commerce Department today will release statistics on new-home sales in July. Economists said these sales probably slipped to a 1.15 million annual pace after a record 1.16 million a month earlier.

Photo: Low mortgage rates in June are credited with brisk sales of existing homes in July. Mortgage rates have risen in recent weeks. (Photo by Bloomberg News)

Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Housing Starts Hit 17-Year High in July
By Bloomberg News
442 words
20 August 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
5
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

Starts on new homes unexpectedly surged last month to the highest level in 17 years, a new report shows, providing further evidence that housing remains remarkably robust.

Builders started work on 1.872 million homes at an annual rate in July, a 1.5 percent increase from June and the most since 1.933 million in April 1986, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. Mortgage rates that have risen more than a percentage point over the last month have not discouraged buyers, construction company executives said.

''We see in our markets a high degree of traffic and optimism,'' said Laurence Hirsch, the chairman and chief executive of the Centex Corporation, a Dallas-based home builder.

But the Commerce Department report also showed that building permits, an indicator of future production, fell 2.4 percent last month to 1.78 million, from 1.823 million a month ago. The drop in building permits was led by authorizations for multifamily projects, which declined 9.8 percent in July.

And consumer confidence might not be quite as strong as previously thought. The University of Michigan's closely watched sentiment index fell to 90.2 in August, from 90.9 in July. A drop in Americans' perception of their financial situation tempered sentiment.

The Michigan university's current conditions index decreased to 100.5 this month from 102.1 in July. That largely reflected a drop in expectations for wage increases and concern about higher gasoline prices in the summer driving season, people with access to the survey said.

Still, the University of Michigan survey, released on Friday, showed an increase of more than 10 points since the end of the Iraq war.

The housing report showed that starts of single-family homes rose 1.9 percent in July to a rate of 1.521 million units, the highest since 1.525 million in November 1978. Starts of multifamily homes declined 0.6 percent to an annual rate of 351,000.

Single-family permits edged down 0.3 percent, to a 1.423 million annual rate from June, which had the highest pace since 1960.

Builders have reported a rise in demand even as mortgage rates rose from June's record low of 5.21 percent. The rate on a 30-year fixed mortgage rose as high as 6.34 percent in early August, according to Freddie Mac, the nation's second-largest mortgage buyer.

In the Region/Westchester
Real Estate Desk; SECT11
Price of Single-Family Home Up Almost 10% in Year
By ELSA BRENNER
1357 words
10 August 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
10
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

THE median price of a single-family home in Westchester reached $570,000 at the end of the second quarter -- up almost 10 percent from a year earlier and close to the highest level ever in the county, according to a report from the Multiple Listing Service. The record, $570,750, was set in the third quarter of last year.

In large part, the prices for single-family homes are being driven up by a lack of inventory, a persistent problem for several years, according to the listing service, a subsidiary of the Westchester County Board of Realtors. The single-family housing stock peaked at 6,699 homes on the market in 1995. The figure fell to a low of 2,296 in 2001 and has edged up since then.

Although inventory by the end of the second quarter was up almost 25 percent from a year earlier, to 3,243 homes from 2,602, it was still not enough to meet demand, real estate agents said.

Very low mortgage interest rates helped to fuel demand for homes, in spite of the sluggish state of the national and state economies in early 2003 (the period that generated the second quarter's closed transactions), the report also said.

Because of the dearth of housing stock, especially in the middle range, ''homes listed in the $500,000's and $600,000's are getting snapped up in bidding wars,'' said Nicholas Wolff, the owner of Century 21 Wolff in White Plains. He mentioned a White Plains four-bedroom, two-bath Tudor on a 50-by-100-foot lot that was listed in mid-July for $539,000. It had an accepted offer of $555,000 within a week, Mr. Wolff said.

In Pleasantville, a four-bedroom, two-bath ranch on 1.81 acres that came on the market last week for $629,000 had four offers within 24 hours, said Donna Conte, the manager for Holmes & Kennedy's Pleasantville office.

''Especially with the economy sluggish and people losing jobs, buyers are more reluctant to spend a lot, to overextend themselves,'' Ms. Conte said. ''That's another reason houses in middle range are at a premium.''

By contrast, houses at the high end -- in the $800,000's and $900,000's in White Plains and $1 million and above in northern Westchester -- are taking longer to sell.

There are currently 187 homes available at $1 million to $2 million in northern Westchester (New Castle, North Castle, Bedford, Lewisboro, Somers, North Salem, Mount Kisco and Pound Ridge), according to the listing service. Fifty-four of them have had their prices cut since going on the market, said Roselyn Harburger, branch manager for Houlihan/Lawrence's Katonah office. ''In this price range, buyers now have more opportunity and choices than in the past,'' she said.

A major concern among brokers and sales agents is that Westchester's high prices for single-family homes have encouraged potential buyers to look in Putnam County to the north, Mrs. Harburger added. The median price of a single family-home in Putnam was $355,000 at the end of the second quarter -- ''what it was in Westchester in the second quarter of 1999,'' she said.

OR as Ms. Conte at Holmes & Kennedy explained, ''Lots of buyers, even those looking at the low end of Westchester's market, are saying, 'Wait a minute; I can't spend that much.' '' Even with low interest rates, the down payment is often an obstacle, especially for first-time buyers, she said.

At least in the southern part of the county, though, buyers still seem willing and able to pay the high prices, especially if they need to be within close commuting distance of New York City, said Liz Lindsey, manager of Houlihan/Lawrence's Bronxville office. ''If they must have Manhattan, they'll stay in the county,'' she said. ''I'm not saying that at some point, we won't see more home buyers moving farther away from the city, but that's not happening as of now.''

Reflecting the low inventory and high prices, the number of closed sales for single-family homes in Westchester dropped 19 percent in the quarter, to 1,165, from 1,444 a year ago, according to the listing service. Similarly, the dollar volume of sales dropped by almost 13 percent, to $861.4 million, from $985.8 million in the second quarter of 2002.

In all residential categories (single-family houses, condominiums, co-ops and multifamily houses), there were 2,041 closed sales in the quarter, down 12 percent from a year ago. The dollar volume of closed sales for all residences was $1.08 billion, down 8 percent from $1.18 billion a year ago.

The only category that showed an increase in the number of sales was condos, up 4 percent, to 309, in the quarter from 298 a year ago. In dollar volume, condo sales were up 15 percent, co-op sales were up 10 percent and two- to five-family houses were up 5 percent.

In the quarter, the median sale price of a condo was up 11 percent, to $300,000, from $270,000 a year ago. The median sales price of a co-op was up 38 percent, to $131,000, from $95,000. And the median sales price of a two- to five-family house was up 28 percent, to $435,000, from $339,000.

According to the listing service's report, there appeared to be ''no sign at all through the second quarter that any sector of the Westchester-Putnam residential real estate market is resting upon a price bubble,'' despite the low inventory and high prices.

The report added that even though interest rates had begun to creep up since the close of the second quarter, ''there is a very long distance to go before such increases become a problem.'' The average rate on a 30-year conventional mortgage was less than 6 percent and approached 5 percent in the first half of the year.

Westchester was not alone when it came to inventory problems in the first half, according to Walter Molony, a spokesman for the National Association of Realtors, a trade organization in Washington.

''There were more buyers than sellers across the country,'' he said. ''Usually, we see a six-month supply. For the past two years, it's been more like a four-and-a-half-month supply.'' The length of supply is determined by how long it will take to exhaust available inventory if houses continue to sell at their current pace, Mr. Molony said. The population -- in particular, the number of new households -- is growing faster than the supply, he said.

Nationally, the median sales price of a single-family home in the first quarter -- the most recent period for which the trade association had figures -- was $161,500, up 7 percent from a year ago. In Westchester in the first quarter, the median sales price was $525,500, up 12 percent from the first quarter of 2002. In the New York metropolitan area, the median sales price for a single-family home in the first quarter was $329,400, up 15.1 percent from a year ago, the association said.

Even in an uncertain economy, residential real estate continues to be a good investment, Mr. Molony said. ''Housing is not a quick-in and quick-out kind of thing,'' he said. ''It's an investment that appreciates over time, usually at the rate of inflation plus one to two percentage points.

''And just because housing prices aren't rising as fast as they were, there's no indication they're going to go south. Some temporary price corrections are always a possibility, but even that's not a likelihood.''

Photo: Asking price for three-bedroom, two-bath split-level at 4 Collyer Place, White Plains, is $629,000, in a price zone with high demand. (Photo by Susan Farley for The New York Times)

The Brookhaven Town Board last week approved a six-month building moratorium on planned retirement communities and multi-family housing developments so the town can complete a study on high-density housing.

Local civic leaders had been calling for a halt to such construction for more than a year, saying the town had approved too many high-density projects in recent years without first developing a town-wide land use plan for them.

Local builders and developers spoke out against the moratorium at a public hearing last week and persuaded the town board to consider amending it to allow projects that have received approval within the last two years but have not yet started construction to go forward during the moratorium period.

Robert A. Wieboldt, the executive vice president of the Long Island Builders Institute, said, ''The town is facing a phenomenal need for senior housing and multi-family housing, but it's getting a lot of resistance to these projects.''

Town officials said the moratorium would affect applications for projects that could create as many as 15,000 new housing units in the town. Mr. Wieboldt said that only about 4,500 of those proposed units are really at risk because ''some of the proposals are just pipe dreams,'' but added that the number of stalled units would cost the town roughly 4,500 jobs. ''It's going to be a major economic loss for the town, and it will create a big hole in production in 2004,'' he said. Vivian S. Toy

Metropolitan Desk; SECTB
Despite a Sluggish City Economy, Apartment Prices Up in Manhattan
By MOTOKO RICH
705 words
16 July 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
6
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

After two consecutive quarters of decline, the average sales price of apartments in Manhattan rose 11 percent, to $864,860, in the second three months of the year despite a sluggish economy, according to a report released yesterday.

Although the increase is nowhere near the 29 percent rise in the average sales price that occurred in the first three months of 2000, it suggests that the Manhattan real estate market is being buoyed by low-interest mortgages and growing consumer confidence.

''I think it's partly attributable to a longer-term confidence and a light at the end of the tunnel about the economy,'' said Jonathan Miller, president of the appraisal firm Miller Samuel Inc. and the author of the report, the Douglas Elliman Manhattan Market Overview. ''While economic conditions certainly aren't robust, they appear to be getting a little bit better.''

The median sales price, which increased to $575,000, almost 12 percent more than the median for the previous quarter and almost 13 percent more than the median for the period a year ago, was at its highest level since the first market overview report was issued in 1989. (The median price is midway between the highest and lowest prices.)

The average and the median sales prices of cooperative apartments were at record highs, with the average up almost 9 percent to $775,052 from the first quarter this year, and the median price at $479,000, also an increase of almost 9 percent.

Peter Kelleher, a director of sales at Douglas Elliman, said the increases belied a general perception about the market. ''Everybody says it's such a strong buyers' market,'' Mr. Kelleher said. ''They may have more choices, but I don't think they're getting reduced prices.''

Indeed, Steven L. James, sales director in Elliman's East Side office, said he was seeing bidding wars again. ''I'm amazed,'' Mr. James said, adding that his office recently sold a four-bedroom cooperative apartment in the East 70's off Park Avenue for $3.8 million after three people competing to buy it drove the price up from a listing price of $3.5 million.

The annual growth in the average price for all apartments was not as striking as the quarterly increase. The average sales price was up more than 4 percent over the average price for the same period last year.

Applying a different measure, the Corcoran Group, which gathers data about signed contracts rather than closed sales, the method used in the Elliman report, said the average sales price actually declined slightly to $840,554 this year, from $872,907 last year. ''Last spring, buyers brought unabashed enthusiasm to the market, and overbidding was commonplace,'' said Pamela Liebman, chief executive of Corcoran. ''This spring has been a healthier market. While there are still certainly plenty of hot deals and bidding wars, on the whole the market is much more rational.''

Ms. Liebman said Corcoran's agents had recently noted a resurgence in the market for apartments priced at $10 million or more.

The Elliman report said sales for all apartments in the second quarter increased almost 11 percent, to 2,055, compared with sales in the first quarter, though that figure represented a decline of nearly 25 percent from the 2,726 apartments sold in the same quarter last year. Mr. Miller said the second quarter of 2002 was particularly strong, as buyers who held back after the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks returned to the market.

Some markets did see strong annual growth. Stephen Kliegerman, executive director of sales for the downtown office of Halstead Property L.L.C., said sales downtown increased about 15 percent in the second quarter compared with sales in the same period last year.

The Elliman report said sales of two-bedroom apartments, about 45 percent of the total sold in the second quarter, were particularly strong, up more than 20 percent over sales in the first quarter. Though sales were down 24 percent from the period last year, Mr. Miller said, the market for two-bedroom apartments was much stronger than the market for studios.

Real Estate Desk; SECT11
Behind the Region's Run-Up in Prices
By JOSH BARBANEL
3436 words
13 July 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

THE value of homes in the New York metropolitan area has risen faster than homes in almost all other areas in the country for more than five years, providing a bounteous reward to anyone with the foresight or luck to already own a home, but raising questions about the impact of the high prices on home seekers and housing policies.

Across the region, the median sale price of a single-family home has risen by 75 percent since 1998, an increase of more than $140,000, according to data collected by the National Association of Realtors. Across the country, prices have risen 26 percent in that time.

Of 139 separate geographic areas tracked in the price survey, the entire New York region ranked fifth in percentage gain. But Nassau and Suffolk Counties on Long Island ranked first in the country during this period, with home prices nearly doubling; the median price of all homes sold in the two counties increased by $162,000, to $338,000. Long Island was followed by the Sacramento and San Diego areas, and then Monmouth and Ocean Counties along the Jersey shore. Bergen and Passaic Counties in northern New Jersey ranked ninth.

At times in recent years, there have been frenzied bidding wars, with houses selling within days if not hours after being placed on the market. But more recently, brokers say, price increases are moderating; supplies of homes for sale -- especially more expensive homes -- are increasing; and the market is becoming what brokers say is ''more balanced,'' albeit at the highest median prices ever.

''The worst you can say about housing prices is that the rate of price increases is slowing,'' said Rae Rosen, a regional economist with the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. ''To me the continued price appreciation reflects the underlying strength of the economy in the metropolitan area. People are voting with their feet. They are saying we are staying here and paying premium prices to do so.''

The surge in prices here during a period of economic weakness is the product of many factors, both national -- notably, extraordinarily low interest rates -- and local, such as a slowing in the construction of new homes, especially moderately priced ones, and a critical lack of land for new home construction.

Together, these trends raise concerns for some homeowners and home seekers. Will prices stagnate, and in some cases fall, as they did in much of the region in the early 1990's after a huge run-up in prices during the previous decade? Will young families today with average middle class incomes be able to afford homes in the region? What will happen if interest rates rise as high as they were a few years ago?

For Patricia Green, a senior assistant dean in the Frank G. Zarb School of Business at Hofstra, the rising housing market worked out just right. After completing her doctorate in educational administration, she was recently hired as an associate dean at Ithaca College in upstate New York and it was time to sell the house she shared with her mother and two dogs.

Her father had purchased a Cape Cod home in the original Long Island Levittown in 1952 for $6,700; it was modestly expanded some time later, but the kitchen had never been updated.

It was put on the market for $299,990, a price her broker, Michael Litzner of Century 21 American Homes, thought would appeal to an entry-level home buyer with income of $80,000 to $100,000. The property attracted immediate interest, since more extensively remodeled Levittown homes cost upward of $100,000 more.

The potential buyers ''were every age, every type of person,'' Dr. Green said. ''There were some single women, some couples with children, some without children, older people, a racial and ethnic mix. Some were moving from condos, some moving to be closer where they worked. I had five buyers on the weekend after Memorial Day, preapproved.''

By the end of Sunday, the house was sold for $300,000, $10 more than the asking price, enough to pay for a much larger house along Cayuga Lake in Ithaca with a mortgage with 5.2 percent interest.

Brokers in many parts of the region say $300,000 homes have gone the way of the 10-cent telephone call. And where homes in this price range are available, they sell quickly and are in short supply, while inventories of much higher-priced homes are rising.

For first-time buyers, the situation can be bleak. ''They call you and you feel bad for them,'' said Sharon Tucker of Tucker Associates GMAC Real Estate in West Nyack in Rockland County. ''They just got married, they are in the city and they have been renting. These homes are tough to find. You might be able to show them three or four homes, and their apartments are bigger.''

A ''decent little house'' in Nanuet is $375,000 to $380,000, she said. Instead, she suggests a condominium in Valley Cottage for $219,000 plus $350 in monthly maintenance and $4,500 in property taxes a year.

A few years back in Clifton, N.J., Diana Bustamante of Keller Williams Team Realty sold a very nice, but small, Cape Cod house on Surrey Lane for $204,000 and, she recalled, ''people thought I was a miracle maker.''

Now, she said, a similar two-bedroom Cape on Lyall Road, a few blocks away, is on the market for $289,000 in a neighborhood that attracts many first-time home buyers, frequently immigrants who had been living in New York City and Hudson County. To find a larger house with a driveway and a garage in a ''nice neighborhood'' is nearly impossible for less than $300,000, she said.

''There are not enough properties on the market for the number of buyers out there,'' Ms. Bustamante said. ''Where were these buyers 10 years ago? We had too many houses and not enough buyers.''

In Mercer County, John Burke, manager of the Gloria Nilson GMAC Real Estate office in Hamilton, said that though the number of multiple offers on properties had declined, they were still common. ''There are times that we scratch our heads trying to justify the prices,'' he said.

Source of the Surge

Dramatic Impact Of Falling Rates

The driving force behind the surge in prices here and across the country has been sharply falling mortgage interest rates, as the Federal Reserve Bank responded to the weakness in the economy by driving rates to their lowest levels in more than 30 years.

It is hard to overestimate the impact of these falling interest rates on the housing market. It was as if the Federal Reserve Bank had put tens of thousands of dollars in the pocket of nearly every home buyer in the country: every qualified buyer could get a larger loan, based on his or her income, at no extra cost.

Mortgage interest rates began rising in the late 1990's as the stock market took off and peaked in May 2000, at a monthly average of 8.52 percent for a 30-year mortgage, before falling to 5.23 percent last month, according to Freddie Mac.

A buyer who put down 20 percent in cash and who was willing to pay $2,000 a month in mortgage payments would have been able to afford a $325,000 house in the year 2000, but today would be able to afford a $454,000 house, a extra $129,000 or 40 percent, in purchasing power over a short period. Of course, the down payment would be almost $26,000 larger.

The lower interest rates enabled many homeowners to refinance their mortgages and either reduce payments or borrow more cash without increasing their monthly payments, or both. And even homeowners who did nothing saw their homes rise in value.

This extra purchasing power brought many thousands of new buyers into the home market. Others rushed to trade up. People who had never been able to qualify for mortgages suddenly were in the market. Prices rose, and across the country many more new homes were constructed.

Economists say the tremendous wealth effect for those who owned homes provided some comfort in the face of stock market losses, including those in 401(k) retirement plans of people who did not otherwise dabble in the stock market, and protected the economy from a deeper recession.

The effect of this can be seen in statistics that track the mortgage payments needed to support the median priced home in the metropolitan area. Despite sharp increases in these prices, the mortgage needed to support the typical home has risen far less, and after controlling for the cost of living has actually declined from the late 1980's.

Since the year 2000, median prices in the New York area have increased 43 percent, but the monthly mortgage payment needed to carry that home increased only 14 percent, to $1,552, and only 7 percent after accounting for inflation.

By June, when mortgage rates fell further, the mortgage on that same median house would have declined by $100 a month. Even more striking, since 1990 prices have increased by nearly 90 percent but mortgage payments have actually declined by 11 percent after accounting for inflation.

New York Region

Where Supply Is Lagging

The interest rate bounty does little to explain why housing prices are rising faster in the New York metropolitan area than elsewhere in the country. To economists, the answer can be summarized by a simple economic proposition: supply and demand.

In much of the country, the supply of housing is expanding to meet the demand. In the New York region and in a few densely populated areas on both coasts that is not happening.

David A. Lereah, the chief economist for the National Association of Realtors, said that the run-up in prices is not evidence of a ''bubble'' similar to the Internet stock market frenzy, but the functioning of a rational marketplace.

''The supply of homes is very, very lean in New York,'' he said. ''That is why you have such huge price appreciation. That is a healthy thing.''

He said that around New York construction of new homes has not kept pace with job creation over the last decade. ''Something has to give,'' he said, ''so prices go up.''

He said that over 10 years, one home has been created for every 1.6 jobs created nationally, but in the New York metropolitan area one home has been created for about every 16 new jobs. He estimated it would take an additional 269,007 homes in the New York area to meet the needs created by a decade of economic growth.

The comparison between the New York region and the Atlanta metropolitan area, which leads the country in new home construction, is stark. In Atlanta, one single-family home building permit was issued for every 81 residents last year, compared with one for every 735 residents in the New York area, including suburban counties in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut, according to the Census Bureau. When multifamily dwellings are included, the ratio shifts to one unit for every 61 residents of Atlanta, compared with one unit for every 378 in the New York region.

The New York area, analysts say, is running out of land for large housing developments. The new homes that are being built -- not infrequently by knocking down older homes -- are marketed to more affluent buyers of large homes.

When a new home is built, it typically frees up an older, lower-priced home that is also put on the market. When fewer homes are built, this trickle-down effect is weaker. At the price range that many first-time homeowners can afford, there are few homes to be had.

Michael H. Schill, a law professor and director of the Furman Center for Real Estate and Urban Policy at New York University, worries that the ''smart growth'' movement in some New York suburbs, which seeks to protect open spaces and limit sprawl in outlying areas through restrictive zoning, is cutting into the supply of land for housing. In theory, high-density developments are supposed to compensate for land left undeveloped, but proposals for such developments are often fiercely resisted.

''If you limit the amount of land that can be used for housing, there is no surer way of getting higher home prices,'' he said. ''It is transferring wealth from renters and people getting into the market to upper income people.''

The demand for lower priced homes may be so extreme and the supply so limited that it is distorting statistics in the New York area and perhaps exaggerating the median price increase, according to some housing analysts and appraisers.

The median price data compiled by the National Association of Realtors is based mainly on prices at closings reported by local multiple listing services used by brokers across the country. It is the most current national housing price survey, although it excludes sales by owners and exclusive sales not reported to the listing services.

Jeffrey G. Otteau, the president of the Otteau Appraisal Group, which publishes market reports for much of New Jersey, said the housing picture ''is not quite as optimistic'' as the closely followed median price figures suggest.

When the New Jersey Board of Realtors recently published figures showing continuing double-digit increases in median prices in Ocean and Monmouth Counties, he concluded that most of the houses sold were new homes in ''luxury price ranges,'' while more down-to-earth homes were hard to find. Thus, the median-price home, with half of all sales higher and half lower, moved upward.

''Most builders wish they could find land for lower priced homes, but municipalities don't want it,'' he said. ''The trend is that most are increasing their minimum lot size requirements.''

He estimated, based on recent transactions, that the true price increases in New Jersey this year will be far lower than in recent years, closer to 5 percent.

The same pattern may hold in Westchester County, where 2,200 building permits were reported last year, including 1,049 for single-family homes. The new construction is barely enough to replace homes lost through fires and demolition, according to P. Gilbert Mercurio, chief executive of the Westchester County Board of Realtors.

In Westchester, he said, inventory was up and sales were down, but, contrary to the usual functioning of supply and demand, median prices remain higher than ever.

Mary Ellen Tolan, a broker in the Scarsdale office of Houlihan/Lawrence, said that inventory is rising for the most expensive properties, while there was little available at the lower end of the market, which in Scarsdale is $500,000 to $600,000. ''There is softness at anything over $2 million,'' she said. ''Sellers have been holding the line, but we are starting to see some compromise.''

A similar pattern is evident in the Manhattan condominium and co-op market. Jonathan Miller, president of Miller Samuel, an appraisal company, said that prices currently seem to be rising over levels of a year ago but that there was a quarter-to-quarter price decline in the first quarter of this year. In that period, prices of the properties in the highest 10 percent of the market fell more, by 11.5 percent, than the overall median, which declined by 1.9 percent.

Looking Ahead

Weighing the Effects Of Increases in Rates

But what happens when mortgage interest rates stop falling and start to rise? Dr. James W. Hughes, dean of the Edward J. Bloustein School of Planning and Public Policy at Rutgers University, remembers the local real estate boom in the early 1980's, followed by years of static and even declining prices in the recession in the early 1990's, and says the current market is risky for anyone who buys now and needs to sell within a year or two.

Although many economists forecast a modest rise in interest rates during the second half of this year, they say that it will probably have little effect on the housing market. If rates rise as part of a broad economic recovery, incomes will rise too, limiting the impact on home prices and values.

Under this theory, buyers will then have to pay more each month to cover the cost of a new home purchase, but will have more income available. Unless the local economy hits some unexpected bumps, they say, the demand for homes by aging baby boomers and immigrant families will probably continue as well.

The lack of inventory of moderately priced homes is having a ripple effect across the region.

In the Bronx, demand is keen but inventory low, according to Joseph F. Limongelli, a broker at Better Homes Realty and president of the Bronx-Manhattan North Association of Realtors.

He said that homeowners in the East Bronx, Throgs Necks and surrounding areas are finding housing in the suburbs more expensive to buy and to own, because of higher taxes, and are staying in the Bronx. In Throgs Neck, a one-family row house is selling for about $310,000, and larger three-bedroom houses for $350,000. ''People are staying put rather than moving to the suburbs,'' he said. ''A lot of them are improving what they have and staying.''

TURF
House & Home/Style Desk; SECTF
For Home Buyers, Fear of Commitment
By MOTOKO RICH
1484 words
3 July 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

LAST year, Jeff Holthaus, a 36-year-old engineer in Chicago, decided he was ready to settle down. But he had a commitment problem. Sure, he had his reasons. First, he had to figure out what he wanted. Then his company decided to move to Michigan, so he had to find a new job. And it wasn't always about him, either. In one case, he was the one who was rejected.

Then, in April, he thought he had found The One, until, at the last minute, he backed out of it: a two-bedroom condo in Bucktown, an up-and-coming neighborhood in Chicago. For Connie Grunwaldt, Mr. Holthaus's real estate agent, it was like watching a bridegroom run away from the altar.

''He sent me over the edge,'' said Ms. Grunwaldt, who said she had showed Mr. Holthaus 92 apartments over the previous year.

Mr. Holthaus, who finally signed a contract in May and moved into a two-bedroom, $271,000 apartment in the Old Town section of Chicago last weekend, said that because it was his first time buying a home, he was ''somewhat apprehensive and worried about having buyer's remorse.'' And having just gone through a job change, ''there was some nervousness due to the economy and the instability of the future.''

He may be extreme, but Mr. Holthaus is not unique. According to Ms. Grunwaldt, buyers with the jitters are on the increase -- so much so that she is now writing up two contracts for every buyer, on average, compared with just one at the same time last year. The faltering is most evident, she said, among first-time buyers. ''They're thrilled with the fact that interest rates are low, and they're jumping on without thinking it through,'' she said. ''Then when they get into the process, they realize, 'Whoa!' ''

Although interest rates are at 45-year lows and the housing market continues to zip along, real estate agents in many cities have noted the emergence of cold feet. Buyers are becoming confused by contradictory economic signals and worry that the housing market may have hit its peak.

For sellers, the hesitation can be more than irksome. David Lew, a 40-year-old investment banker in Manhattan, said that five different times since April, he thought he had sold his one-bedroom co-op apartment in Murray Hill. Each time, he accepted an offer and sent out a contract.

Mr. Lew, who has already moved with his wife to a two-bedroom Midtown apartment they bought last summer, has been surprised by all the stumbles. ''It's contrary to what I had heard before I put my apartment on the market,'' he said.

''I was reading that the one-bedroom market is holding up'' because of low interest rates, he added. ''But it's the intangibles, like 8 percent unemployment in New York and the economic uncertainty that has led to what's going on now. It's frustrating with all this last-minute disappointment.'' Mr. Lew said he had accepted the fifth offer a week ago, but yesterday his real estate agent, Daniel Ruiz of Brown Harris Stevens in Manhattan, said the buyer had backed out.

Mr. Ruiz said in an earlier interview that buyers are starting to perceive a shift in the market. ''There's this sense that people can walk away from things and there is plenty of other stuff on the market,'' he said. In May, he said, out of 14 accepted offers that his office handled, only 7 went to closing; in June, 8 out of 17 deals fell apart.

In the San Francisco region, some real estate agents report a rise in the number of buyers pulling out of deals. Jeff Sterley, a broker with Pacific Union Residential Brokerage in suburban Greenbrae, Calif., said that 30 percent of accepted offers are now being broken, up from 20 percent a year ago and 10 percent three years ago.

And in New Jersey, Jay Schweppe, chief executive of Schweppe & Company Realtors of Montclair, the number of deals that fall apart is up 50 percent since last year. ''When you put the pressure of a six-year run of appreciation, all of a sudden people are asking, 'Am I buying at the top of the marketplace?' '' Mr. Schweppe said.

In some cases, it's not the buyers who are pulling out, but their bankers. In Brockton, Mass., near Boston, Stephen Damon, office manager of Jack Conway Real Estate, said three of his deals had fallen apart in the past month because the lenders decided at the last minute that the buyers' finances did not qualify them for a mortgage. ''When it comes down to the nitty-gritty, sundry things happen, and they're denied,'' Mr. Damon said. By now, the low interest rates have drawn most of the financially stable buyers into the market, and some with shakier credit histories are trying to sign up for mortgages.

In New York, another deal-breaking factor is the co-op board. While many buyers can get a mortgage, they may not have the wherewithal to pass stringent board requirements.

Carol Shainswit, a broker in the Midtown office of the Corcoran Group, said she is representing the seller of a three-bedroom apartment on the Upper East Side who has had to turn away three offers because the buyers were unlikely to pass muster in the building.

Indeed, many brokers report that co-op boards have gotten tougher over the past year or two and that rejections are up. ''Buildings pull up the gangplank when they're worried that the financial environment is touchy,'' said Frederick W. Peters, president of Ashforth Warburg Associates in Manhattan.

Some boards are asking for liquid assets that are two or three times the purchase price of the apartment. Evelyn Cole, a broker at Stribling & Associates, said that some Upper West Side buildings were raising their requirements for the cash portion of a deal to 35 percent, from 25.

The real estate markets are by no means collapsing. In May, the last month for which figures are available, sales of existing homes rose to 5.92 million units, 4.4 percent more than in May of last year, according to the National Association of Realtors in Washington. Historically low mortgage rates continue to drive the market, and in many areas, properties favored by entry-level buyers are still generating bidding wars.

But while many first-time buyers are driven by the attractive trade-off between rents and mortgage payments, buyers hoping to move to larger homes are frightened by the shaky economic recovery.

Late last month, for example, the owners of a three-bedroom apartment on the Upper East Side thought they had sold the unit for $2.7 million when the buyers, a couple with three children who wanted a bigger place, decided to pull out. Peter Marra, president of the William B. May real estate agency, said the husband called and said: ''You know what, a couple of guys in my department were let go, and I'm concerned. This is a big commitment, so I'm just going to stay where I am for a while.''

Jon Hyde, a 61-year-old retired advertising executive in northern California, said such jitteriness nearly drove him mad when he sold his family's home recently. By the time he moved out of the house this week, three separate would-be buyers had tied it up in escrow for several weeks each before pulling out of the deals.

Each time he received an offer for the place, a three-bedroom secluded wood house in the San Rafael hills that he had owned for 19 years, he asked to meet the buyers and show them around the property.

''It was a pleasure for me to meet the next owner of my house,'' Mr. Hyde said. But after three failed deals, he said, ''it got to the point where I would have sold it to bin Laden.''

''Of course,'' he added, ''I would have turned him in.''

In the end, Mr. Hyde sold the house to a divorced man for $1.18 million, down from an original asking price of $1.25 million. This time, Mr. Hyde stayed out of the process. As he put it, ''I felt I was in a position where I had to lower the price, be flexible, keep my mouth shut and stay out of the way.''

Photos: NOT SO FAST -- David Lew, left, thought he had sold his New York co-op -- five times. Jeff Holthaus, above, toyed with sellers in Chicago before settling down in a two-bedroom condo. (John A. Brooks for The New York Times; left, G. Paul Burnett/The New York Times)(pg. F8)

BRIEFINGS: DEVELOPMENT
New Jersey Weekly Desk; SECT14NJ
HOUSING PLAN APPROVED
By Karen DeMasters
136 words
22 June 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
6
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

A $500 million housing and commercial development proposed for the site of the Garden State Park racetrack in Cherry Hill will begin construction this summer with the settlement of a two-year court battle over the number of affordable housing units in the project.

Judge Theodore Z. Davis of Superior Court signed an agreement last week that gives Florida-based Turnberry Cherry Hill LLC permission to build 1,659 housing units, including 285 for low- and moderate-income families, said Rick Ochab, a spokesman for the developer. The complex also will include 582 units for people 55 and older, as well as several retail businesses.

The Garden State Park, located on 223 acres, ran its last horse race in May 2001. Karen DeMasters

In the Region/New Jersey
Real Estate Desk; SECT11
Home Prices Still Rising Except at the High End
By ANTOINETTE MARTIN
1559 words
15 June 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
2
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

OH, what a beautiful spring, comes the chorus from home sellers. While the rain never seemed to stop falling, prices went as high as an elephant's eye in many suburbs and some say they will keep climbing way up the sky.

Well, perhaps that is putting a bit of extra gloss on realtors' unstintingly upbeat views of the New Jersey home market. There is, many of them will concede, a cloud or two on the horizon when it comes to sales of the most expensive homes. But real estate professionals agree the trend in sales prices continues upward for homes with asking prices below about $700,000 -- at least in communities with short commutes to Manhattan, at least while interest rates remain at record lows.

''We had 15 percent appreciation in sales prices across the board for homes we listed and sold last year,'' said Pat Hoferkamp, president of Burgdorff ERA. ''This year, to date, we've again had 15 percent appreciation.''

She noted that Burgdorff has 23 offices in towns along commuter train lines in northern New Jersey, which makes them ''very desirable communities.'' Still, 30 percent appreciation is robust any way you look at it, Mrs. Hoferkamp said, and she sees no signs of slackening.

She and other residential brokers said they perceived no price bubble that will abruptly burst -- even in towns where prices seem to have floated to the stratosphere.

IN Montclair, where last year one home got 22 offers and sold for $298,000 -- or 42 percent -- over the asking price, the average offer is running 5 to 10 percent over asking price this spring, according to local realtors, and bidding wars seem to have become virtually part of the landscape.

Last weekend, the Sunday open houses were mobbed as usual, for homes ranging from a restored and upgraded Craftsman colonial offered at $1.4 million to a spiffed-up three-bedroom Dutch colonial on the market for $429,000.

A three-bedroom, one-bath home across the street from the Dutch colonial -- virtually untouched for approximately 50 years, and being sold on behalf of its 91-year-old owner with no marketing other than a sign -- garnered four offers the weekend before. It sold for $366,000 -- 8 percent over the asking price.

Burgdorff ERA's Montclair office manager, Carolee Jones, said there were 13 immediate offers on another home in the same Upper Montclair neighborhood, with the top bidder offering $150,000 over the asking price for a house that had been listed at more than $500,000.

''I think the rapid appreciation can continue indefinitely,'' said Coldwell Banker's Montclair office manager, Robert W. Norman, ''primarily because interest rates are so low.'' Mr. Norman said that based on typical mortgage computations, buyers who could afford to pay $1,800 a month on a 30-year mortgage loan would have been able to afford a $450,000 house two years ago -- and now, with interest rates a percentage point lower, could afford a $600,000 house.

Montclair was one of four New Jersey towns listed this month by Money magazine as being among places in the nation where median home values have increased the most in the last five years.

An analysis performed by a consultant for the magazine said the median value of homes increased 102.5 percent in that period in Montclair, 102.9 percent in the shore town of Rumson, 92.7 percent in Glen Ridge -- which borders Montclair -- and 92.5 percent in Palisades Park.

But Jacelyn Botti, president of Weichert Realtors, the largest residential firm in the state, observed that five-year figures never reflect what is happening right now, which is, as she sees it, this: ''Prices are increasing more rapidly in the most affordable markets, and the rate of appreciation is beginning to slow in average and median price homes.

''You can take communities and neighborhoods with the lowest household prices in an area or a town, and they are still experiencing the highest rate of appreciation,'' she said. ''The average and median home sale prices are being impacted by an extremely soft upper-end market, for homes priced at $2 million-plus.''

In Rumson, where median home prices rose to $493,500 at the beginning of this year, the high-end market has slowed somewhat, said Coldwell Banker's office manager, Bob Kievit, who is also the president-elect of the New Jersey Association of Realtors. ''Anything in decent condition priced around $500,000 is not going to last,'' Mr. Kievit said, ''but homes selling for a million dollars and up are now taking a few months to sell, and prices are a bit lower than they would have been six months to a year ago.''

The slower market for the highest priced homes is true across the state and actually began more than two years ago with the decline of the stock market and the halt in production of ''under-30 millionaires,'' according to Jeffrey G. Otteau, who runs a New Jersey home appraisal consultancy bearing his last name. The average age of buyers in the $1 million-and-up range has gone up by about 10 years in the last couple of years, Mr. Otteau said, adding that the statement was based on information gleaned by regularly polling sales agents of newly constructed homes. ''Buyers in the older age range are much more cautious,'' he said.

The slowing in the upper end market began at a time when the overall market was still ''very very tight,'' he said -- meaning there were many more buyers than homes available. ''But it takes a couple of years of that to see an effect on price,'' said Mr. Otteau. ''Remember that in the 1980's, when we last saw a price correction, the market began to weaken observably in '86-'87, but it was not until '89 that the supply of homes for sale swelled to the point where we saw an effect on price.''

Ms. Botti of Weichert agreed with other residential specialists that there are pockets where the market continues to be ''extremely strong,'' with price appreciation of 20 percent or more. ''In those markets, there is still lack of inventory for the eight-four-two colonial,'' she said, meaning a limited number of ever-popular eight-room houses with four bedrooms and two baths.

But, Ms. Botti said, based on the statistics Weichert keeps -- and the multiple listing services and other numbers she is privy to -- the number of homes for sale across the state is sharply up. ''I haven't seen listing inventory levels of this size for three and a half to four years,'' she said. ''We see houses staying on the market longer, and more of them are 'expiring,' '' she said, meaning that sales agents are unable to broker a sale within the time span of a sales contract, typically six months.

She and others attributed this in part to the impact of the spate of reports about rising prices, with those reports causing more and more homeowners to put homes on the market, which will inevitably lead it to slow down.

Mr. Otteau added that many baby boomer homeowners are putting homes on the market because they have decided it is time to downsize or migrate full-time to the Jersey shore.

IN the hottest markets in the state -- those with direct commuter service to Manhattan or quick access to the shore or both -- sales agents said that the heat radiates out from any particular town's boundaries. For example, a renovated three-bedroom home on Parkview Drive in Bloomfield near Brookdale Park, which straddles Montclair's border with Bloomfield, was sold in March for $509,000 -- $110,000, and 32 percent, over the asking price -- no longer shocking in Montclair, but a first for Bloomfield, as best area realtors said they can recall.

Mr. Kievit said he bought a modestly priced house in a town near Rumson and has seen its value rise on a pace with Rumson's. ''Little tiny homes in Ocean Beach -- 600 square feet, on 50 by 30 foot properties -- are selling for $250,000 to $300,000 right now,'' he said

Where might it all end? Some buyers are beginning to sour on the auction atmosphere in the Montclair market, various realtors said, and are turning to other towns in the area. ''That's a big reason why we see the big hike in appreciation in Glen Ridge,'' Mrs. Hoferkamp said.

But Mr. Kievit said he simply cannot foresee an end to rising prices. ''This area has always been strong,'' he said of Monmouth and Ocean Counties, both of which hug the shore. ''There is only one ocean, and only one financial center to commute to in Manhattan. Short of Wall Street moving to Idaho, I think it will always be an attractive place to live.''

Photo: This renovated three-bedroom home on Parkview Drive in Bloomfield sold in March for $509,000, which was $110,000 over the asking price. One realtor said that prices have risen 15 percent since the beginning of the year on top of a 15 percent increase last year. (Dith Pran/The New York Times)
