Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Prices of Homes Are Still Rising, But More Slowly [CORRECTED}
By DAVID LEONHARDT
1510 words
3 June 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

CORRECTION: A chart in Business Day on Tuesday showing the rise in home prices in the first quarter of 2003 for parts of New York, California, Florida and Massachusetts carried an incorrect label from a document provided by the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight for increases during a longer period. The gains were for the last five years, not since 1980.

WASHINGTON, June 2 -- The growth of house prices has slowed sharply over the last year, increasing by the smallest amount in five years during the first three months of 2003, according to a government report released today.

But the report relieved some of the growing fears that the housing market was a bubble beginning to burst, particularly in California, the Northeast and other places where home values soared over the last decade. During the first quarter, prices fell in only 13 of the 220 metropolitan areas ranked by the government, down from 22 during the fourth quarter of last year and 33 during the third quarter.

''No one has anything to be concerned about in the immediate future,'' said Shelly Dreiman, a senior economist at the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight, which compiles the data. Over the next few years, ''it depends on what happens with the economy,'' she added.

Avoiding a housing slump is crucial to the economic recovery that most forecasters are predicting for the rest of this year, they say. Even the current slowdown in prices could damp economic growth, however, by reducing the amount of money people receive after they sell their homes or refinance their mortgages.

Nationwide, house prices rose at an annual rate of 3.8 percent during the first three months of 2003, down from 5.4 percent at the end of 2002 and 8 percent at the start of last year, the government said.

The biggest recent price increases have come mostly in places that are on the outskirts of big metropolitan areas and have become havens for people unable to afford homes closer to the city. Between early 2002 and early 2003, the price of a typical home jumped at least 13 percent on Long Island; in Dutchess County and Newburgh, N.Y.; in Santa Barbara, Calif.; in Providence, R.I.; and in Port St. Lucie, Fla., which is north of Miami.

In the New York metropolitan area, prices rose at an annual rate of almost 9 percent during the first three months of the year, up slightly from late last year.

The 13 areas where prices fell during the first three months of 2003 were a mixture of once-booming technology centers, like Boulder, Colo., and San Jose, Calif., and cities in the country's thinly populated middle, like Kalamazoo, Mich., and Jackson, Miss.

But the decreases were small and have been occurring for only a few months. Over the last 12 months, prices rose in all 220 metropolitan areas ranked by the government -- even in the industrial city of Kokomo in northern Indiana, where the increase was less than 1 percent, the smallest for any ranked area.

Housing has become an increasingly important part of the economy over the last decade as the percentage of Americans who own their homes has risen and changes in the banking business have allowed people to refinance their mortgages more easily. The rapid price increases since 2000 have allowed many households to spend money more quickly than their incomes alone would have permitted, lifting growth at a time when businesses have been cutting their spending.

But few economists thought that annual house price increases of 7 percent to 8 percent would continue for very long. Instead, they have wondered whether many local markets had turned into bubbles that would pop like the stock market or whether the price increases would merely slow.

''You've got a tug of war going on,'' said Karl Case, a professor of economics at Wellesley College.

''We're not gaining jobs, and the economy is still pretty much in the doldrums,'' he said. ''But there are two things going for the housing market: interest rates are very low, and there's nowhere else to put your money.''

California, Florida and the Northeast have attracted the most attention as bubble suspects because prices have risen 50 percent to 75 percent in many places there over the last five years. But other than in San Jose, which is in Silicon Valley, prices continued to rise during the first three months of 2003, despite a worsening of the economic slump that is now more than two years old.

In both the Los Angeles and Miami metropolitan areas, prices rose at an annual rate of 7.9 percent. The increase was 6.1 percent in the Washington area, 5.8 in the Boston area and 2.9 percent in the San Francisco area, according to the government data, which compares a house's sale price to its previous sale price.

Over the last year, the biggest increases occurred in the Redding, Calif., and Chico, Calif., areas, both of which are in the north-central part of the state.

The study does not include the top end of the housing market because it is based on information collected by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, two government-chartered mortgage agencies that rarely handle loans larger than $322,700. Prices of the most expensive homes have fallen in some areas, real estate agents say.

But the declines appear to be isolated. Data compiled by both Fiserv CSW, a research company in Cambridge, Mass., and the National Association of Realtors also show that house prices continued to rise in almost every metropolitan area over the last few months. The two groups analyze homes in all parts of the market.

In the first three months of the year, the median home -- the one worth more than half of all houses and less than half -- cost $161,500, up 7 percent from early 2002, according to the Realtors' group. With a median price of $509,000, the San Francisco area had the most expensive homes, followed by Orange County, Calif., at $448,000, and Boston, at $414,000. The median price in the New York area was $329,000.

Photo: Data showing house prices still growing, albeit slowly, so far this year have eased fears of the housing market being a bubble about to burst. (Tim Boyle/Getty Images)(pg. C5)

Confidence and Home Sales Rose Solidly Last Month
AP
741 words
28 May 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
5
English
(c) 2003 New York Times Company

WASHINGTON, May 27 -- Low mortgage rates beckoned buyers in April and propelled sales of new homes to the highest level this year and sales of previously owned homes to the fifth-best month on record, reports released today showed.

The Commerce Department reported that sales of new single-family homes rose 1.7 percent from March to April, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.03 million. That was the best showing since December.

In a second report, sales of previously owned homes rose 5.6 percent in the same period to an annual rate of 5.84 million, the fifth-highest level on record, the National Association of Realtors, a trade group, said.

Both reports provided fresh evidence that the housing market continues to be one of the few bright spots for the economy. ''Low mortgage rates are keeping the housing market red hot,'' said Joel Naroff, president of Naroff Economic Advisors.

A third report, from the Conference Board, a private research group, showed that consumer confidence rose in May.

The Consumer Confidence Index rose to 83.8 in May from 81 in April, when the index jumped 19.6 points as consumers cheered the swift end of heavy fighting in Iraq. It was the highest level since a reading of 84.9 in November. Analysts had projected an index of 84 for May.

''The postwar euphoria experienced last month has quickly given way and consumers' focus has returned to matters on the home front,'' said Lynn Franco, director of the group's Consumer Research Center.

The performance for sales of new homes and existing ones surprised economists. They were predicting new-home sales to drop 2.7 percent and existing-home sales to rise to a rate of 5.70 million.

In March, new-home sales jumped 8.2 percent, while existing-home sales fell 5.6 percent.

Low mortgage rates drove home sales to record levels last year, and this year is shaping up to be second-best for sales of existing homes and new ones, economists say.

The average rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage in April was 5.81 percent, compared with 6.99 percent for the same month a year ago.

Rates on 30-year mortgages dropped to a new low of 5.34 percent last week, Freddie Mac, the mortgage giant, said. It was the seventh time this year that this benchmark mortgage rate fell to a weekly low.

''With interest rates near rock-bottom, it is really a why-not-buy deal,'' said David Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders.

By region, in the Midwest, new-home sales jumped 13.4 percent in April from the month before, to an annual rate of 186,000. In the West, sales rose 4.3 percent, to a rate of 267,000, and in the South they went up 0.6 percent, to a rate of 487,000. In the Northeast, however, sales fell 17.8 percent, to a rate of 88,000.

For previously owned homes -- the biggest chunk of the housing market -- sales rose 9.6 percent in the West, to an annual rate of 1.60 million in April. In the Northeast, existing-home sales went up 4.8 percent, to a rate of 660,000, and in the South, sales increased 4.1 percent, to a pace of 2.31 million. In the Midwest, sales also rose 4.1 percent, to a rate of 1.27 million.

Home Sales And Orders To Factories Show Declines
AP
560 words
27 March 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Page 6, Column 1
English
c. 2003 New York Times Company

WASHINGTON, March 26 -- Sales of new single-family homes plunged to the lowest level in nearly three years in February, while manufacturing demand weakened, according to two reports released by the Commerce Department today.

''The reports suggest that February was a very weak month for the general economy,'' said Lynn Reaser, chief economist at Banc of America Capital Management. ''We saw the negative effects of bad weather, worries about an impending war with Iraq, an increase in oil prices and a decline in the stock market.''

New-home sales plummeted 8.1 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 854,000, the lowest level since August 2000. That came on top of an even steeper 12.6 percent decline in January.

There was a 36.8 percent drop in the Northeast, which was hard hit by snowstorms during the month. That pushed sales in the region down to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 48,000, the lowest since January 1996.

Sales in the South fell 8.6 percent, to a rate of 395,000, and in the Midwest they went down 6.3 percent, to a pace of 149,000. In the West, sales were flat -- at a rate of 262,000.

''The weather is a problem and so is the weight of uncertainty on consumer behavior,'' said David Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders. ''I'm assuming both are quite temporary.''

In the manufacturing report, orders to factories for big-ticket goods fell 1.2 percent in February, unwinding part of the 1.9 percent gain reported in January.

Private economists had predicted orders for durable goods -- products expected to last at least three years -- would fall as companies put off buying new equipment with war imminent.

The weakness in orders to factories was broad based, with losses reported for computers, cars and metals.

''There does not seem to be enough traction in the economy for the manufacturing segment to sustain forward momentum,'' said Daniel Meckstroth, chief economist at the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, a research group.

The manufacturing sector, hardest hit by the 2001 recession, has been the biggest drag on the economy's ability to return to full throttle.

In the durable-goods report, orders for automobiles fell 1.5 percent in February after a 9.9 percent advance in January.

Excluding orders for transportation equipment, durable goods orders declined 2.1 percent in February, the largest drop in eight months.

Orders for computers plunged 12.3 percent after a 1.2 percent rise in January. For primary metals, the category including steel, orders fell 2.3 percent after January's 2.8 percent increase.

Shipments, a good barometer of current demand, decreased 1.6 percent in February after a 2.7 percent gain in January.

Low Interest Rates Help Keep Home Sales Brisk in February
Bloomberg News
327 words
26 March 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Page 7, Column 1
English
c. 2003 New York Times Company

WASHINGTON, March 25 -- Americans, undeterred by impending war and encouraged by record low mortgage rates, bought existing homes in February at a brisk pace, the National Association of Realtors reported today.

Homes sold at an annual pace of 5.84 million units, down from a record 6.1 million in January, the association said. Higher sales levels occurred only in January 2002, when the pace was 5.96 million and in December 2002, at 5.91 million.

The low mortgage rates are expected to buoy sales of houses this year as well, economists said. Because home sales are counted when the transactions are completed, the totals reflect purchases arranged in previous months, before heavy February snowstorms discouraged shopping, consumer confidence reached decade lows and unemployment edged higher.

''Generally strong sales are expected this year, assuming the war in Iraq is not prolonged,'' said David Lereah, chief economist for the Realtors' association. At the same time, he said, ''the fragile economy combined with geopolitical concerns clouds the outlook,'' and last month's storms will probably reduce March sales.

Economists had forecast that sales of existing homes would decline to a 5.8 million annual pace.

The supply of existing homes available for sale, another gauge of housing demand, fell to 4.4 months' worth in February, from 4.5 months in January.

The median price of an existing home rose 0.9 percent in February, to $161,600, from $160,200 the previous month. The price was up 8.2 percent from a year earlier.

In the Midwest, sales rose 4.8 percent, to an annual pace of 1.3 million. In the South, sales decreased 8 percent, to a pace of 2.3 million. Resales in the Northeast dropped 2.9 percent, to 680,000, and in the West they declined 6 percent, to a 1.56 million annual pace.

Economic Data Point in Both Directions
AP
652 words
28 February 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Page 3, Column 1
English
c. 2003 New York Times Company

WASHINGTON, Feb. 27 -- Sales of new homes plunged in January at the fastest pace in nine years, while orders for durable goods posted the biggest rise in six months, reports from the Commerce Department indicated today.

The sale of new single-family homes fell to a seasonally adjusted annual pace of 914,000, a 15.1 percent drop from December, when sales reached a record monthly level.

The performance, much weaker than analysts had expected, represented the worst single month for new-home sales since January 1994, and, economists said, raised some new questions about consumers' willingness to make big financial commitments amid worries about a war in Iraq. Other economists, minimizing the importance of the decrease, cited the cold weather and seasonal distortions.

Still, ''you can't dismiss sales being off by 15 percent,'' said David Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders. ''I think we are seeing more caution.''

Home sales fell sharply in all parts of the country, except the Northeast, where they rose. Sales in the Midwest dropped 42.2 percent in January, to a pace of 163,000. In the South, sales fell 12.9 percent, to 406,000, and in the West, they dipped 1.4 percent, to a pace of 279,000. But in the Northeast, sales rose 43.5 percent, to a pace of 66,000.

In a separate report, the Commerce Department said orders to factories for durable goods, relatively costly items expected to last at least three years, rose 3.3 percent in January. The increase came after orders fell 0.4 percent in December and 1.2 percent in November.

It surpassed the 1 percent advance that economists had predicted and was the strongest performance since July.

The gains were broad based. Orders for transportation products rose 5.1 percent in January, in contrast to December's 3.5 percent decline.

Orders for automobiles increased 10.7 percent, the largest increase since April, and a turnaround from a 5.9 percent drop in December.

Excluding orders for transportation products, which can swing widely from month to month, orders for durable goods rose 2.5 percent in January.

Orders for computers rose 1.5 percent, down from a 10.4 percent advance in December. For communications equipment, orders soared 46.1 percent, more than erasing a 15.8 percent decline in December.

Machinery orders rose 5.3 percent, after dropping 1 percent. Orders for primary metals, including steel, grew 4.2 percent, after a 2.6 percent rise.

In still another report, the Labor Department said new claims for unemployment benefits rose by a seasonally adjusted 11,000 last week, to 417,000, a two-month high, suggesting that businesses were inclined to keep work forces lean.

The four-week moving average, which irons out some weekly fluctuations, also rose, to 399,750, from 395,750 the previous week.

c. 2003 New York Times Company

WASHINGTON, Feb. 3 -- Construction spending was brisk in December as home building hit a record, but manufacturing slowed in January, according to two reports released today.

In one report, the Commerce Department said that a 1.2 percent increase in spending pushed the value of all construction projects to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $858.3 billion. That came after a 0.9 percent gain in November, the agency said, and was the biggest increase in 10 months.

Analysts had been expecting a gain of 0.3 percent.

Home building has been the main source of strength for construction.

In December, spending on all residential projects rose to a record $434.6 billion on an annualized basis, the agency said, a 2.9 percent increase from November.

While the residential side has been strong, analysts say, the commercial side of the construction market has remained weak, reflecting a reluctance by businesses to make big commitments in capital spending, including new factories and other buildings.

Spending on commercial projects by private builders fell 1.9 percent in December from November, the agency said, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $158.6 billion.

The drop was led by declines in spending for office buildings. Spending was also reduced for hotels and motels, another segment of the construction market that is still feeling the lingering effects of the 2001 terrorist attacks.

Spending on big government projects in December rose 1 percent to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $210.5 billion, the highest level since February.

For all of 2002, construction spending rose 0.4 percent, a big slowdown from the 2.7 percent gain in 2001. Last year's performance was the worst since 1991.

In another report, manufacturing slowed in January.

The Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing activity slipped to 53.9, unable to sustain December's momentum when the index jumped to a revised 55.2.

A level above 50 indicates the manufacturing sector is expanding; below 50 means it is contracting.

Still, January's performance was a bit better than analysts expected. They were forecasting a reading of 53.

Chart: ''Construction Spending'' Total construction spending in billions of dollars at a seasonally adjusted annual rate. Monthly Change Dec. -- +1.2% Nov. -- +0.9% Oct. -- +0.9% Graph tracks construction spending since July 2001. (Source: Commerce Department)

Letters to the Editor
Westchester Weekly Desk; Section 14WC
Affordable Housing And 'Rural Character'
449 words
2 February 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Page 13, Column 1
English
c. 2003 New York Times Company

To the Editor:

Councilwoman Jessica Bacal's letter to the editor (Jan. 26) regarding affordable housing in the town of Lewisboro asserts that crediting the town with only 8 units toward its 129-unit share of the county's need is misleading.

In 1990, taking into account all the existing affordable housing in Westchester, the County Board of Legislators determined the need for an additional 5,000 units in the decade of the 1990's. Therefore, no pre-existing housing can be deemed to satisfy any portion of that goal.

Ms. Bacal suggests that the town can meet its obligation by relying on accessory apartments. Since accessory apartments are usually created by subdividing an existing house without changing its external appearance, they would have a lesser effect on the town's ''rural character'' than new units would.

To be ''affordable'' and count toward the goal, an apartment must be subject to affordability controls for 20 years, on both the income of those who live in them and the rents charged by their owners. The accessory apartments in the town are not only not subject to affordability controls, but their permits are also subject to discretionary re-approval by the town every two years.

Since accessory apartments are allowed only if owners continue to live in the house, it must be assumed that they will be free to choose the kind of household that will be allowed to share it. The probability that this will lead to the exclusion of families with children and a variety of other applicants has motivated the Housing Opportunity Commission's policy of allowing not more than 25 percent of the municipality's obligation to be satisfied in this manner.

At five units per acre, the town's entire affordable housing obligation could be satisfied by new construction on less than 26 of the town's 18,740 acres! How much of an impact could that have on the town's ''rural character''? On the other hand, the affordable housing shortage is almost universally recognized to be the county's No. 1 problem. Therefore, blind devotion to the preservation of the town's ''rural character'' by limiting development to mansions on large lots and by ignoring the broad socioeconomic impacts of continued exclusionary zoning evinces a regrettable myopia, which has been condemned by the state's highest court. It seems no more than reasonable to expect all town board members to modify ''the Lewisboro way'' of doing things to reflect the fact that the town is not ''an island entire of itself.''

GEORGE M. RAYMOND

Chairman

Westchester County

Housing Opportunity Commission

Business/Financial Desk; Section C
Orders for Durable Goods and Sales of New Homes Show Gains
By Reuters
540 words
29 January 2003
The New York Times
NYTF
Page 6, Column 3
English
c. 2003 New York Times Company

Orders for big-ticket manufactured goods rose modestly in December while sales of new homes increased sharply, according to two government reports released yesterday.

The Commerce Department said orders for durable goods like aircraft and machinery edged up 0.2 percent in December, helped by military spending. But that was less than the 0.9 percent gain economists had expected, and excluding the military, orders fell 0.2 percent, pointing to subdued business investment ahead.

The meager December gain followed a revised drop of 1.3 percent in the previous month and had many economists talking about weak gross domestic product growth in the fourth quarter, probably below 1 percent.

Orders for nonmilitary capital goods excluding aircraft, viewed as a proxy for business capital spending, fell 0.1 percent after a 3.1 percent drop in November, bolstering the view that companies are holding off their spending.

''The report shows to us that capital spending was sluggish in the fourth quarter and is not providing much momentum going into the first quarter of this year,'' said Rick Egleton, deputy chief economist at BMO Financial Group in Toronto.

In a bright spot for the technology sector, orders for the broad category of computer equipment rose 3.2 percent, the largest increase since July. While orders for computers and related products surged, communications equipment orders sank.

In a separate report, the Commerce Department said sales of new homes increased by more than a million annualized units for a fifth consecutive month. They rose 3.5 percent in December, to an annual rate of 1.082 million units, easily topping forecasts of 1.045 million units, which was also the revised November rate.

Sales of newly built homes in 2002 totaled 976,000, the highest since the Commerce Department began keeping the statistics in 1963.

A third report, from a private research group, the Conference Board, showed its consumer confidence index fell to a nine-year low of 79.0 in January, from a revised 80.7 in December.

While consumer confidence has fallen for seven of the last eight months, the drop was more moderate than consensus forecasts of a 78.1 reading and consumers' assessment of their present situation actually improved a bit.

The drop in January consumer confidence was driven by a drop in consumers' expectations for the next six months. By contrast, most consumers reported that their situation had improved in the last month.

The present situation index, a measure of consumers' current attitudes about the economy and their finances, rose to 75.4 in January, from a revised 69.6 in December. But the expectations index, a gauge of consumers' six-month outlook, fell to 81.4 from a revised 88.1.

Dec. -- 0.2% Nov. -- 1.3 Oct. -- 1.7 Graph tracks new orders since July 2001. (Source: Commerce Department) Chart: ''Consumer Confidence'' Index measuring attitudes toward the economy, based on 1985 -- 100.
